    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2363128047599462543">Firmin is a rat born in a book (a shredded copy of Finneggans Wake), who finds the books he consumes also consume his soul. He becomes a vagabond and philosopher, struggling with mortality and meaning.<br/><br/>In the basement of a Boston bookstore, Firmin is born in a shredded copy Finnegans Wake, nurtured on a diet of Zane Grey, Lady Chatterley’s Lover, and Jane Eyre (which tastes</span>
  <span id="freeText2363128047599462543" style="display:none">Firmin is a rat born in a book (a shredded copy of Finneggans Wake), who finds the books he consumes also consume his soul. He becomes a vagabond and philosopher, struggling with mortality and meaning.<br /><br />In the basement of a Boston bookstore, Firmin is born in a shredded copy Finnegans Wake, nurtured on a diet of Zane Grey, Lady Chatterley’s Lover, and Jane Eyre (which tastes a lot like lettuce). While his twelve siblings gnaw these books obliviously, for Firmin the words, thoughts, deeds, and hopes—all the literature he consumes—soon consume him. Emboldened by reading, intoxicated by curiosity, foraging for food, Firmin ventures out of his bookstore sanctuary, carrying with him all the yearnings and failings of humanity itself. It’s a lot to ask of a rat—especially when his home is on the verge of annihilation.<br /><br />A novel that is by turns hilarious, tragic, and hopeful, Firmin is a masterpiece of literary imagination. For here, a tender soul, a vagabond and philosopher, struggles with mortality and meaning—in a tale for anyone who has ever feasted on a book…and then had to turn the final page. <br /><br />First published by Coffee House Press in 2006. Republished by Delta, a division of Random House, in 2009.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2363128047599462543" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/11386532" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/11386532?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Molly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Molly" href="/user/show/728981-molly">Molly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer11386532" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6779699726239137188">Again, I must be a voice of dissent here. I loved this novella. It is written from the perspective of a rat who was born in a bookstore to an alcoholic-mother in a litter of other rat-like rats. Firmin is of course different from his brothers and sisters: he possesses a yearning for knowledge and a loneliness that he obliquely recognizes as the loneliness of the human condition--particularly among those humans whose lives he vicariously observes. This makes him a liminal figure at home neither i</span>
  <span id="freeText6779699726239137188" style="display:none">Again, I must be a voice of dissent here. I loved this novella. It is written from the perspective of a rat who was born in a bookstore to an alcoholic-mother in a litter of other rat-like rats. Firmin is of course different from his brothers and sisters: he possesses a yearning for knowledge and a loneliness that he obliquely recognizes as the loneliness of the human condition--particularly among those humans whose lives he vicariously observes. This makes him a liminal figure at home neither in the world of rats nor in the world of humans. Rather than veering into existential and/or overtly philosophical territory, however, the plot remains clever and turns in part on the unreliability of Firmin's claims and narration. This makes the book both more philosophical and more entertaining than the premise might otherwise suggest. I highly recommend it as story that successfully marries Kafka's Josephine with Disney's Ratatouille.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6779699726239137188" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1397108625" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1397108625" />

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9049915-james-barker?shelf=somehow-reviewed">somehow-reviewed</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1397108625" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9058383699624630124">I adored this intelligent, quirky book. Yes, it's told from the POV of a rat but this is no 'Secret of Nimh.' The rat in question, Firmin, is the runt of a litter of 13 and, because this mother only has 12 nipples he has to play 'musical teats'.. with little success. Instead he has to sustain himself by nibbling classic books. Birthed by an alcoholic mother in the basement of a bookshop in Boston's Scolloy Square there are, thankfully, enough volumes to keep him full. But, in allowing the words </span>
  <span id="freeText9058383699624630124" style="display:none">I adored this intelligent, quirky book. Yes, it's told from the POV of a rat but this is no 'Secret of Nimh.' The rat in question, Firmin, is the runt of a litter of 13 and, because this mother only has 12 nipples he has to play 'musical teats'.. with little success. Instead he has to sustain himself by nibbling classic books. Birthed by an alcoholic mother in the basement of a bookshop in Boston's Scolloy Square there are, thankfully, enough volumes to keep him full. But, in allowing the words to gestate in his belly, Firmin the Vermin soon finds he prefers to read words than eat them. He gets through shelves and shelves of books of all types faster than any goodreader. The act of reading provides self-help but also a troubling melancholy that does not befall his lowly, rat brothers and sisters (quite a metaphor there for those who read and those who don't). They soon flee the bookshop basement for the alcohol-drenched alleys of the on-its-last-legs Square, all squalor and strip clubs, blue cinemas and the beaten. <br /><br />Firmin is lonely and projects on the bookshop owner, Norman Shine. But there is another human who might just offer Firmin the fraternity he needs.<br /><br />Set against the backdrop of the last days of Scolloy Square (before it was demolished and rebuilt as a government area), each chapter starts with a facsimile of the title page of a classic novel. There are also sweet illustrations of the reading rat and his 'sadventures.' Short and not always sweet, the book has moments of quiet comedy and tragedy like any life.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9058383699624630124" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_103186689" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Olivia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2225792-olivia"><img alt="Olivia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1247006886p2/2225792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/103186689?book_show_action=true">May 17, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Olivia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Olivia" href="/user/show/2225792-olivia">Olivia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385342650" href="/book/show/4446359-firmin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2225792-olivia?shelf=contemporary-literature">contemporary-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer103186689" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3791947269525038313">This book really surprised me. When I saw it at Powell's Books, I knew right away I was the target audience. See that white chunk on the right side of the cover? It's a literal chunk taken out of the book. As though a rat had nibbled it. On the back, it said it was a book about Firmin, a rat who begins by consuming literature, but soon finds it consumes him. It's about a rat who loves books. Perfect for me. So I bought it, guessing it would be a fun, cute summer read, with little substance.<br/><br/>It is</span>
  <span id="freeText3791947269525038313" style="display:none">This book really surprised me. When I saw it at Powell's Books, I knew right away I was the target audience. See that white chunk on the right side of the cover? It's a literal chunk taken out of the book. As though a rat had nibbled it. On the back, it said it was a book about Firmin, a rat who begins by consuming literature, but soon finds it consumes him. It's about a rat who loves books. Perfect for me. So I bought it, guessing it would be a fun, cute summer read, with little substance.<br /><br />It isn't cute, and it certainly isn't lacking in substance. Rather, it is a beautiful exploration of a lonely genius. I couldn't help but identify with Firmin the rat as he navigated the human world, separated by his lack of voice and the fear most humans hold for rats. <br /><br />The cast of characters is rounded out by a bookshop owner, a compassionate (but probably off-his-rocker) sci-fi author, Fred Astaire and Ginger Rogers (who are residents of Fermin's fantasy life), and hundreds of books. In his isolation, the books Firmin reads become his companions, adapting to fit into his world. Perhaps my favorite line is the following:<br /><br />"And I introduce people. I put Baudelaire on the raft with Huck and Jim. It did him a lot of good."<br /><br />I loved it only a few pages in, but I also realize that I would probably find it pretentious if it wasn't about a rat. As it is, it's the perfect balance between profundity and humor. Very dark humor, by the way. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3791947269525038313" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer11535974941547963140">Firmin is a story of a rat who lives in a bookstore, eats books (words) and understands the text of the stories he reads. When the bookstore is torn down to make way for urban renewal he goes in search of a new home.<br/><br/>Firmin is, at it's core, an outsider story about someone who never really fits into the world in which he finds himself. <br/><br/>I have a theory about helping people find books. I think all of us are ultimately searching for our story recreated and retold in many different ways, so my person</span>
  <span id="freeText11535974941547963140" style="display:none">Firmin is a story of a rat who lives in a bookstore, eats books (words) and understands the text of the stories he reads. When the bookstore is torn down to make way for urban renewal he goes in search of a new home.<br /><br />Firmin is, at it's core, an outsider story about someone who never really fits into the world in which he finds himself. <br /><br />I have a theory about helping people find books. I think all of us are ultimately searching for our story recreated and retold in many different ways, so my personal goal when I match people and books is to understand the story they identify with. The longer I know someone the better I get at finding just the right book. <br /><br />A small novel, it is a bit different and will be something that a lot of people don't get. A few however, will really identify with with this story. It is also a book that a reader who want to challenge himself will really appreciate because it is so different.</span>

<div id="review_36803781" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36803781" />
    <a title="Stephanie Griffin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/514234-stephanie-griffin"><img alt="Stephanie Griffin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1191879872p2/514234.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36803781?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie Griffin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie Griffin" href="/user/show/514234-stephanie-griffin">Stephanie Griffin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385342650" href="/book/show/4446359-firmin">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone who has a soul.</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/514234-stephanie-griffin?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36803781" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12692871265912674103">This amazing book, written by first-time novelist Sam Savage, blew me away with its intelligent writing and perceptive looks into the human condition. Yes, it’s from a rat’s perspective. Don’t let that deter you from reading one of the best books of the past two years.<br/>Firmin discovers that he lives in a bookshop basement in a run-down part of a city. He nibbles on the pages of the books, but also learns to read voraciously. He closely observes the world of the bookshop and ventures out to nearby</span>
  <span id="freeText12692871265912674103" style="display:none">This amazing book, written by first-time novelist Sam Savage, blew me away with its intelligent writing and perceptive looks into the human condition. Yes, it’s from a rat’s perspective. Don’t let that deter you from reading one of the best books of the past two years.<br />Firmin discovers that he lives in a bookshop basement in a run-down part of a city. He nibbles on the pages of the books, but also learns to read voraciously. He closely observes the world of the bookshop and ventures out to nearby buildings. <br />Firmin is born like any other being, naked and unknowing. His exploration of his surroundings and his attachment to things of familiarity ring true of the way we grow. His awareness of his own psyche is often humorous. His inability to converse with humans, an enormous obstacle to him, is poignant. His false belief that he has a connection with other beings is heartbreaking. All along, Firmin never stops trying.<br />This is a book I will re-read whenever I feel I’m getting too caught up in day-to-day chores. It will remind me to keep reaching out, no matter what the consequences. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12692871265912674103" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review36803781">

However, I found some parts of the book dragging for too long, more than once making me feel sleepy... <br/><br/>On the other hand, all the references to books were interesting, the account of the slow degradation of the neighborhood is very well achieved, the friendship of Firmin with the unsuccessful writer is f</span>
  <span id="freeText15658156147739883488" style="display:none">This book had all the ingredients to make me like it a lot and yet, it was just a little above the OK read. <br /><br />A rat that lives in a bookshop and learns to read is undoubtedly an interesting theme. However, I found some parts of the book dragging for too long, more than once making me feel sleepy... <br /><br />On the other hand, all the references to books were interesting, the account of the slow degradation of the neighborhood is very well achieved, the friendship of Firmin with the unsuccessful writer is funny and touching and even the end of the story, though a bit sad, couldn't probably be very different. But for some reason, which I still haven’t quite realised what it is, this book hasn’t really resonated with me.</span>

Don't get me wrong, I did enjoy parts of it. The author can comment a lot on the true nature of humans by disguising it as natural animal rat instinct - t</span>
  <span id="freeText11276345150573186585" style="display:none">There is some genius behind this story, but unfortunately, it is a genius I do not fully appreciate. What started out as a desire to read a grown-up version of Ratatouille, turned into a much more contemplative Kafka Metamorphosis-esque narrative about literally being a low-life (a rodent) surrounded by humans occupying the same social status. Don't get me wrong, I did enjoy parts of it. The author can comment a lot on the true nature of humans by disguising it as natural animal rat instinct - that is the genius that I did silently "bravo", but in general, it was a little too dark and somber for what I had expected.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385342650" href="/book/show/4446359-firmin">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/173161-lindsay?shelf=adultfiction">adultfiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/173161-lindsay?shelf=literaryfiction">literaryfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer71167286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer312759792931545651">Beautiful, tragic, and wildly creative. Firmin is a rat who is born on a shredded copy of Finnigan's Wake in the basement of a bookshop, and consequently falls in love with literature. Stuck in a sea of rats too simpleminded to understand his intellectual depths and humans he cannot communicate with, he struggles to find happiness.<br/><br/>This book made me laugh, cry, and think. But mostly, it spoke to my soul. I would recommend it to anyone who has ever found solace within the pages of a book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2867778283323360089">It's not an all-time classic, but it has something. <br/><br/>I liked the pictures of the little mousy-looking rats at the bottom of the pages -- the little dears. <br/><br/>I also liked the bite out of the top of the book, which reflects the way Firmin, the first-person narRATor, devours books, both literally and metaphorically.<br/><br/>Where it doesn't quite (sorry) bite is in the character of the narrator, which is not sufficiently distinctive.   <span id="freeText2867778283323360089" style="display:none">It's not an all-time classic, but it has something. <br /><br />I liked the pictures of the little mousy-looking rats at the bottom of the pages -- the little dears. <br /><br />I also liked the bite out of the top of the book, which reflects the way Firmin, the first-person narRATor, devours books, both literally and metaphorically.<br /><br />Where it doesn't quite (sorry) bite is in the character of the narrator, which is not sufficiently distinctive. I like the situation; I like the idea; but the voice reminded me of a number of other fictional voices, but not so good. Dimly I had in my mind <i>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/24384.The_Cricket_in_Times_Square" title="The Cricket in Times Square by George Selden" rel="nofollow noopener">The Cricket in Times Square</a>

</i> and <i>The Mouse and His Child</i> -- but the central character doesn't come through as vividly as in any of these, and not all of them are first person.<br /><br />Still, refreshing, pleasurable, good Christmas read...</span>

    <a title="Lavinia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1405967-lavinia"><img alt="Lavinia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1221135421p2/1405967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/70325630?book_show_action=true">Sep 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lavinia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lavinia" href="/user/show/1405967-lavinia">Lavinia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70325630" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12479472740118709191">Living among (and devouring) books, possessing an enormous general knowledge, sipping coffee and wine, going to cinema, eating peanut butter, living in a writer's house, listening to good music and playing piano; tell me who wouldn't like that? Firmin is everything the average man cannot be, that's why we like him. So he's funny, witty, falsely thinking that others understand him (don't we all?) but his bohemian existence ends sadly and beyond Scollay Square's demolition I can see Savage's own d</span>
  <span id="freeText12479472740118709191" style="display:none">Living among (and devouring) books, possessing an enormous general knowledge, sipping coffee and wine, going to cinema, eating peanut butter, living in a writer's house, listening to good music and playing piano; tell me who wouldn't like that? Firmin is everything the average man cannot be, that's why we like him. So he's funny, witty, falsely thinking that others understand him (don't we all?) but his bohemian existence ends sadly and beyond Scollay Square's demolition I can see Savage's own disillusion when this actually happened. <br /><br />***<br /><br /><i> Un barbat dintr-un bar m-a intrebat odata ce gust au cartile, "asa, in general". Aveam un raspuns deja pregatit, insa ca sa nu se simta complet aiurea, m-am facut ca ramin citeva clipe pe ginduri, inainte de a-i raspunde: "Draga prietene, data fiind prapastia ce desparte toate experientele tale de ale mele, pot sa-ti spun ca nu exista decit o singura aroma aparte care se apropie cit de cit de aceea a cartilor, si anume aroma de cafea. "</i><br /><br />:D</span>
  <a data-text-id="12479472740118709191" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer2452883133879723029">3.5 stars<br/><br/>As soon as I saw that Firmin is a story about a rat born in a bookshop, I knew I had to pick it up. And I got a very quirky and crazy, but also slightly nostalgic little book. I guess you can read it however you want, but I like the idea that the book describes the person of a reader and reading as a very solitary thing, and I quite identified with that. Of course, ever since I discovered the community of readers online and I met a few wonderful book lovers in real life, I can share my </span>
  <span id="freeText2452883133879723029" style="display:none">3.5 stars<br /><br />As soon as I saw that Firmin is a story about a rat born in a bookshop, I knew I had to pick it up. And I got a very quirky and crazy, but also slightly nostalgic little book. I guess you can read it however you want, but I like the idea that the book describes the person of a reader and reading as a very solitary thing, and I quite identified with that. Of course, ever since I discovered the community of readers online and I met a few wonderful book lovers in real life, I can share my experience of reading, but I still do believe that it's a very individual experience. A hundred people could read the same book and each one of them could experience it differently and have a different opinion, and it's both wonderful and frustrating (in cases, where you would like someone else to see and experience what you did, but it's just not possible to fully share it). <br /><br />I enjoyed the mixture of philosophical elements in this book and the lightness showed in the more humorous parts, that made for a really unusual experience. I would recommend it if the summary piques your interest, but be prepared for something different and unique :)</span>
  <a data-text-id="2452883133879723029" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2738896386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8947317516684936260">"It was soon painfully clear that when he looked at me what he mainly saw was a cute animal, clownish and a little stupid, something like a very small dog with buckteeth. He had no inkling of my true character, that I was in fact grossly cynical, moderately vicious, and a melancholy genius, or that I had read more books than he had."<br/><br/>I loved this book, because this was a book for me. It's for people who love books -- sci-fi paperbacks as well as the classics -- but are still somewhat cynical abou</span>
  <span id="freeText8947317516684936260" style="display:none">"It was soon painfully clear that when he looked at me what he mainly saw was a cute animal, clownish and a little stupid, something like a very small dog with buckteeth. He had no inkling of my true character, that I was in fact grossly cynical, moderately vicious, and a melancholy genius, or that I had read more books than he had."<br /><br />I loved this book, because this was a book for me. It's for people who love books -- sci-fi paperbacks as well as the classics -- but are still somewhat cynical about their importance. Or maybe, cynical about their own importance, and love books because they're not important either. It's about the inherent sadness of life, and the incredible joy that can be had in remembering the quietly good moments and the times that were good even if it wasn't a specific moment at all. It's about being an outsider. It's about a rat who seems like he could be a real rat, that if you could watch him you might not notice anything out of the ordinary. It's just really clever and quiet and good, without ever getting too caught up in its own cleverness. Recommended for everyone who wants "I don't know... something different to read."</span>
  <a data-text-id="8947317516684936260" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Jeffrey" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9181646-jeffrey"><img alt="Jeffrey" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1335834864p2/9181646.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2759972824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16317929533719752223">This is a masterpiece that many people have never heard of. Firmin is a rat living in a Boston bookstore. He eventually learns to read. The author, the late Sam Savage, has Firmin take the reader on a literary tour-de-force. Firmin is better read than most people I know, including me.<br/><br/>Although Firmin is a rat, I quickly identified with him. Not the tail and yellow teeth, but his intelligence, his philosophy of life, his taste as a reader, and his empathy for the humans he lives with. His attempts</span>
  <span id="freeText16317929533719752223" style="display:none">This is a masterpiece that many people have never heard of. Firmin is a rat living in a Boston bookstore. He eventually learns to read. The author, the late Sam Savage, has Firmin take the reader on a literary tour-de-force. Firmin is better read than most people I know, including me.<br /><br />Although Firmin is a rat, I quickly identified with him. Not the tail and yellow teeth, but his intelligence, his philosophy of life, his taste as a reader, and his empathy for the humans he lives with. His attempts to convince humans that he is both harmless and a philosopher are both comical and sad.<br /><br />This is a rat, that if I saw him, I think I would try to make friends with. Any creature who loves James Joyce and Ford Maddox Ford can be a friend of mine. Plus, he's a fan of Ginger Rogers and Fred Astaire. You cannot go wrong with that.<br /><br />I highly recommend this book.<br /></span>

<div id="review_976056946" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/976056946" />
    <a title="Eva Sanchez" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7178857-eva-sanchez"><img alt="Eva Sanchez" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544594729p2/7178857.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/976056946?book_show_action=true">Jun 23, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Eva Sanchez" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Eva Sanchez" href="/user/show/7178857-eva-sanchez">Eva Sanchez</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer976056946" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12472689636108689248">A letter I wrote to Sam Savage but couldn't figure out where to send it. <br/>To Sam Savage,<br/>I was walking through the aisles of my local library branch with no idea of what book I wanted to read. I bided my time because it was the afternoon and that meant that my house was hot and humid and the library was not. I skimmed the aisles of stacks of rows of books and plucked them one by one off the shelves. I took in title upon title and tried to guess what a cover art had to do with the descriptions. You</span>
  <span id="freeText12472689636108689248" style="display:none">A letter I wrote to Sam Savage but couldn't figure out where to send it. <br />To Sam Savage,<br />I was walking through the aisles of my local library branch with no idea of what book I wanted to read. I bided my time because it was the afternoon and that meant that my house was hot and humid and the library was not. I skimmed the aisles of stacks of rows of books and plucked them one by one off the shelves. I took in title upon title and tried to guess what a cover art had to do with the descriptions. You can imagine what a long task this was, but it is summer and a girl like me needed a hidden book to praise. I felt the pressure to get going as my sister came to stand at my side and I pulled out a book to which I had never reacted so unexpectedly before. That book was yours; that book was Firmin.<br />Upon feeling the jagged edges in it's design and seeing the cover, I gasped, thinking the book was falling apart in my hands. I took a step back and took a better look at it and my sister and I laughed and ignored the signs to be quiet. I read the back, read the front, and tucked the book into the crook of my arm.<br />I checked the books out, went home, and Firmin was the one I carried in my bag the next day to work. I cracked it open, it amazes me how many books at my library are never used and abused. Have all of the adults in my town grown out of reading? I began the book not knowing what to expect. I closed the book everyday not knowing what to anticipate in the next paragraph. I finished your book in a few short days and realized that it would weigh heavily on my mind if I didn't find a way to write to you and tell you this: Thank you for writing this book.<br />You see, I don't often find books I enjoy, I don't often find authors with a thought process as grand as yours. You taught me so much and Firmin is a great book about those who love books, those who love those people who love books, those who write them, those who sell them! All in a condensed black book of 164 pages.<br />It brings a tear to my eye when I think about it because books and the worlds they contain can be insignificant in it's binding- but sometimes, a book can mean the world to somebody. Books were Firmin's world and if it weren't for his discovery of them, he would have been just another rat scurrying in an alleyway. There's all this meaning I took from his rodent life! You took a cranny in a bookshop, a lonely rat nest, a dust bowl, and gave it life. It has flourished in this book and I am glad I picked it up.<br />Can I tell you that one of my favorite aspects of your book was when you would describe the entirety of other books? Can I tell you that I think it is interesting the realness that came along with this book- your research- the life you have lived? I don't think there could be a more perfect union.<br />I just wanted you to know how I felt about this book. I will be a vagrant and place this book with the cover facing out so that others will see it and know that I want them to read it. I want this book to have finger stains, and a cracked spine. I want this book to learn the grooves on the hands of others and I want others to read the life of Firmin. Contrary to what Firmin said about his piano during the wrecking of his home: "I imagined it giving forth a last tiny sound of its own, and no one would hear it." I want all my friends to hear about this.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12472689636108689248" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer902550290" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13413022497938602388"><a href='http://bookwormscloset.com/firmin-adventures-of-a-metropolitan-lowlife/' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397899486i/9343754._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>Shoes: Lola Ramona<br/>The Beginning: I had always imagined that my life story, if and when I wrote it, would have a great first line.<br/><br/>What a pleasant surprise this book turned out to be! It was an adorable read and a wonderful companion on my trip to London. We follow the rat, Firmin, who lives in a bookshop and develops a taste for books. Literally! He reads and eats books:<br/><i><br/>I had discovered a remarkable relation, a kind of preestablished harmony, between the taste and the literary quality of a bo</i></span>
  <span id="freeText13413022497938602388" style="display:none"><a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/firmin-adventures-of-a-metropolitan-lowlife/" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1397899486i/9343754._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Shoes: Lola Ramona<br>The Beginning: I had always imagined that my life story, if and when I wrote it, would have a great first line.<br><br>What a pleasant surprise this book turned out to be! It was an adorable read and a wonderful companion on my trip to London. We follow the rat, Firmin, who lives in a bookshop and develops a taste for books. Literally! He reads and eats books:<br><i><br>I had discovered a remarkable relation, a kind of preestablished harmony, between the taste and the literary quality of a book. To know if something was worth reading, I had only to nibble a portion of the printed area.<br></i><br>It’s a universal story about loneliness, friendship and the love of literature, but it’s wrapped in originality. I fell for Firmin (try saying that five times fast!) and his story. Firmin is a classic tale of wishing to be someone else. Firmin wants to be beautiful and smart. He wants people to take him seriously, to consider him human. He always imagines himself as a human, trying to copy what he sees in movies and read in books. I particularly loved the scene where he plays the piano for the human he lives with:<br><i><br>… sitting on the bench, swaying to the music, I looked exactly like Fred Astaire, and I sang like him too. Sure, I knew that this was true only from a certain perspective, and that all Jerry heard was a high ratlike squeak. But he loved it just the same. The first time I played and sang for him he laughed till tears ran down his cheeks.<br></i><br>I reacted just the same way and Firmin would have hated it. But I couldn’t help it. I never thought a rat would touch my heart. But there’s a true bookworm for you: throw in some books and I’m your bitch.<br><i><br>Lives in stories have direction and meaning. Even stupid, meaningless lives, like Lenny’s in “Of Mice and Men,” Acquire through their places in a story at least the dignity and meaning of being Stupid, Meaningless Lives, the consolation of being exemplars of something. In real life you do not get even that.<br></i><br><br>For more reviews and book talk, please visit <a href="http://bookwormscloset.com/firmin-adventures-of-a-metropolitan-lowlife/" rel="nofollow noopener">The Bookworm's Closet</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="13413022497938602388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_53656399" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53656399" />
    <a title="Colleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/185680-colleen"><img alt="Colleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1474644199p2/185680.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53656399?book_show_action=true">Apr 22, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Colleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Colleen" href="/user/show/185680-colleen">Colleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53656399" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10226646330667934738">This is, without a doubt, one of the most unusual books I've ever read. At first glance, I thought it was a childrens book. It's definitely NOT a childrens book. It's an adult novel about...a rat. This rat, Firmin, identifies with humans more than other rats. This is due to the fact that as a baby, and as the runt of the litter, he had to turn to eating books because he couldn't compete with his bigger, greedier brothers and sisters for food. Ingesting these books caused him to learn how to read</span>
  <span id="freeText10226646330667934738" style="display:none">This is, without a doubt, one of the most unusual books I've ever read. At first glance, I thought it was a childrens book. It's definitely NOT a childrens book. It's an adult novel about...a rat. This rat, Firmin, identifies with humans more than other rats. This is due to the fact that as a baby, and as the runt of the litter, he had to turn to eating books because he couldn't compete with his bigger, greedier brothers and sisters for food. Ingesting these books caused him to learn how to read. He became a voracious reader even after he lost his taste for books. OK, you say, why would I want to read a book about a rat? Well, there's so much more to this book. It's laugh-out-loud funny. It's clever. It's weird. There are lots of references to books that will make you say, "Oh yeah, I read that." The characters are memorable. The story is accompanied by little illustrations that aren't even particularly well-done, but they add so much to the story. Most of all, though, there's a pervading sense of deep sadness throughout this book. I know that might seem surprising but this book is full of surprises. It's about the destruction of a formerly glorious part of Boston. It's about the sad sacks of society. It's about the awful plight of rats. Even Fermin, as unusual of a rat as he is, still craps on his best friend, Jerry's, pillow and bites his finger. In the end, he's still a...rat. But you'll never love or feel more sorry for a rat than in this book. The end will not be good for Fermin; you know it, he knows it. But Savage surprises you again and Fermin's story comes full circle.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10226646330667934738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText9257997817682381554" style="display:none">Mr Sam Savage sounds like someone out of a Jim Dodge story. His author bio informs us that he has bachelor's and doctoral degrees in Philosophy from Yale University, that he briefly taught at that same venerable institution, and that he has also been a bicycle mechanic, carpenter, commercial fisherman, and letterpress printer. I've just seen his photo now too, and he even looks like someone out of a Jim Dodge story:<br /><br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/200335.Sam_Savage">http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/...</a><br /><br />But whereas you might expect such a man to write a rather straightforward, masculine, Hemingwayan novel about wilderness, dusty plains, squinting into the sun, bare dry branches, callused hands, rocks, carts, mongrels, etc, instead Mr Savage has written - very, very ably - a tender, witty, thoughtful, surprising, delightful book about books, writing and life. And rodents.<br /><br />I can't imagine how any book lover's life could fail to be enriched by the addition of Firmin to one of its knotholes.<br /><br />Favourite quote:<br /><br />"Infested is an interesting word. Regular people don't infest, couldn't infest if they tried. Nobody infests except fleas, rats, and Jews. When you infest, you are just asking for it."</span>

<div id="review_48697930" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/48697930" />
    <a title="Jeff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/166787-jeff"><img alt="Jeff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222352072p2/166787.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/48697930?book_show_action=true">Mar 09, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeff" href="/user/show/166787-jeff">Jeff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789507315725" href="/book/show/4446361-firmin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer48697930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3376717707430040487">Sam Savage’s wildly original novel, the story of a rat born on a copy of “Finnegan’s Wake” in a Boston bookstore, is a tender, beautiful gift to bibliophiles! <br/><br/>Unlike his siblings who feast on the pages of books in blissful ignorance, Firmin not only consumes the books surrounding him in his birthplace, but also becomes consumed by them – their thoughts, their words, and their ideas. He has the “gift of lexical hypertrophy,” but is, unfortunately, trapped by the physical limitations of being a ra</span>
  <span id="freeText3376717707430040487" style="display:none">Sam Savage’s wildly original novel, the story of a rat born on a copy of “Finnegan’s Wake” in a Boston bookstore, is a tender, beautiful gift to bibliophiles! <br /><br />Unlike his siblings who feast on the pages of books in blissful ignorance, Firmin not only consumes the books surrounding him in his birthplace, but also becomes consumed by them – their thoughts, their words, and their ideas. He has the “gift of lexical hypertrophy,” but is, unfortunately, trapped by the physical limitations of being a rat. His mind is, therefore, swimming with thoughts that he is unable to communicate. Left without the ability to speak, sign, laugh, cry, or even sing, he becomes a melancholy clown, a perpetual dreamer. It is to Sam Savage’s credit that he can turn one rat’s obsession with books, Fred Astaire movies, and pornography into a compelling and touching portrait of a reader who is both empowered and defeated by his very obsessions. “Firmin” may be a story about a rat, but it is told with more humanity than most other books I have read over the past year.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3376717707430040487" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText10173408842683668653" style="display:none">This book was very slow. However this book falls flat. It references obscure book titles, for when Firmin meets anything new he labels it with a book title. Nothing really happens in the story, the rat is born and learns to read but never really does anything with his knowledge. He's never able to affect the outside world, so it ended with me asking why I read it because I like a clear problem and some sort of solution. If you like vaguely historical, vaguely sexual, and vaguely pretentious then this is the book. </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51834250?book_show_action=true">Apr 07, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51834250" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8903626039506110288">Okay, it's about a rat who reads (and eats) books and has an active fantasy life. Weird as it sounds, I loved this book. If anything, it was just too sad (given the topic, really, that makes sense, but it hit some of my particular buttons right now). I had trouble reading it at times (and it's really short) due to that fact. It's not all dark, though, and while I'm not sure about the ending, but I actually think I'll be thinking about this book for a while and the chacter of Firmin in particular</span>
  <span id="freeText8903626039506110288" style="display:none">Okay, it's about a rat who reads (and eats) books and has an active fantasy life. Weird as it sounds, I loved this book. If anything, it was just too sad (given the topic, really, that makes sense, but it hit some of my particular buttons right now). I had trouble reading it at times (and it's really short) due to that fact. It's not all dark, though, and while I'm not sure about the ending, but I actually think I'll be thinking about this book for a while and the chacter of Firmin in particular.</span>

<div id="review_51680816" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/51680816" />
    <a title="Johari" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2101858-johari"><img alt="Johari" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436888921p2/2101858.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/51680816?book_show_action=true">Apr 06, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johari" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johari" href="/user/show/2101858-johari">Johari</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385342650" href="/book/show/4446359-firmin">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer51680816" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14525862756556511326">The main character is a rat. A rat who suffers from despair because he can never be more than a rat. A rat who reads to escape his existence. A rat who imagines and creates fantasies in order to survive his undesired lot in life. If you are a person who cannot handle anthropomorphism, try and read the book as metaphor for the outsiders/others that live on the fringes of civilized societies.<br/><br/></span>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Xtr7Qw9VwD6I67pxQw4DMz/kSR7YkbmtdyuCjEgj7F5F4M6XP/XtTYYNZJJVCdVuxedtuno3HuQgcSNWcuKZdQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('sVvGeffIjgNC74pBqwq5utM5LG2qV4mwkMgLhOSRBAiqYfOtx2ijcEwJVKK9DW/nKToIyQjxLvnHkqpe3lBxIw==')})
  }

The primped and perfumed Amsterdam burghers came to me in droves requiring cures for fevers, love balms, the miscarriage of a b<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText14249762799187794088\" style=\"display:none\">&quot;I, Jeronimus, am a man of phials, a measurer of powders on bronze scales, a potion brewer, an opium and arsenic merchant. The primped and perfumed Amsterdam burghers came to me in droves requiring cures for fevers, love balms, the miscarriage of a bastard child, and, of course, poisons. Ah, poisons.&quot; So speaks Jeronimus Cornelisz, a thirty-year-old apothecary who transforms before our eyes into a murderous madman.  The Company is a novel based on the 1629 voyage of the Dutch East India Company flagship Batavia, bound for the colonies with a cargo of untold riches. Among the passengers is Cornelisz, a man ousted from polite society by sordid rumors of necromancy. Corrupt to the very marrow of his soul, Cornelisz considers himself God\'s equal, the rightful heir to gold, silver -- even another man\'s wife. So twisted is he by lust and greed that he incites a mutiny, running the ship aground on a reef.  All is lost -- the ship is wrecked, its passengers dying, the treasure trashed at the bottom of the sea. &quot;The apothecary will heal us,&quot; the survivors pray, believing themselves lucky to be alive. In the name of benevolence, Cornelisz seizes command of their island refuge. The brave castaways stir with hope -- until the killing begins. For forty frenzied days, Cornelisz decides who shall live and who shall die, leaving his victims with just one wish -- that they had gone down with the ship.  Soaked with the blood of the innocent and the wicked, The Company plunges, with the weight of history, deep into the heart of darkness.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"14249762799187794088\" href=\"#\" <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10509799559342868395">Sam Savage was an American novelist and poet. He was a native of South Carolina living in Madison, Wisconsin. He received his bachelor and doctoral degree from Yale University where he taught briefly, and also worked as a bicycle mechanic, carpenter, commercial fisherman, and letterpress printer.</span>
